Rust comes with a variety of things in its standard library. However, if\\n4: //! you had to manually import every single thing that you used, it would be\\n5: //! very verbose. But importing a lot of things that a program never uses isn't\\n6: //! good either. A balance needs to be struck.\\n7: //!\\n8: //! The *prelude* is the list of things that Rust automatically imports into\\n9: //! every Rust program. It's kept as small as possible, and is focused on\\n10: //! things, particularly traits, which are used in almost every single Rust\\n11: //! program.\\n12: //!\\n13: //! # Other preludes\\n14: //!\\n15: //! Preludes can be seen as a pattern to make using multiple types more\\n16: //! convenient. As such, you'll find other preludes in the standard library,\\n17: //! such as [`std::io::prelude`]. Various libraries in the Rust ecosystem may\\n18: //! also define their own preludes.\\n19: //!\\n20: //! [`std::io::prelude`]: crate::io::prelude\\n21: //!\\n22: //! The difference between 'the prelude' and these other preludes is that they\\n23: //! are not automatically `use`'d, and must be imported manually. This is still\\n24: //! easier than importing all of their constituent components.\\n25: //!\\n26: //! # Prelude contents\\n27: //!\\n28: //! The items included in the prelude depend on the edition of the crate.\\n29: //! The first version of the prelude is used in Rust 2015 and Rust 2018,\\n30: //! and lives in [`std::prelude::v1`].\\n31: //! [`std::prelude::rust_2015`] and [`std::prelude::rust_2018`] re-export this prelude.\\n32: //! It re-exports the following:\\n33: //!\\n34: //! * <code>[std::marker]::{[Copy], [Send], [Sized], [Sync], [Unpin]}</code>,\\n35: //!   marker traits that indicate fundamental properties of types.\\n36: //! * <code>[std::ops]::{[Fn], [FnMut], [FnOnce]}</code>, and their analogous\\n37: //!   async traits, <code>[std::ops]::{[AsyncFn], [AsyncFnMut], [AsyncFnOnce]}</code>.\\n38: //! * <code>[std::ops]::[Drop]</code>, for implementing destructors.\\n39: //! * <code>[std::mem]::[drop]</code>, a convenience function for explicitly\\n40: //!   dropping a value.\\n41: //! * <code>[std::mem]::{[size_of], [size_of_val]}</code>, to get the size of\\n42: //!   a type or value.\\n43: //! * <code>[std::mem]::{[align_of], [align_of_val]}</code>, to get the\\n44: //!   alignment of a type or value.\\n45: //! * <code>[std::boxed]::[Box]</code>, a way to allocate values on the heap.\\n46: //! * <code>[std::borrow]::[ToOwned]</code>, the conversion trait that defines\\n47: //!   [`to_owned`], the generic method for creating an owned type from a\\n48: //!   borrowed type.\\n49: //! * <code>[std::clone]::[Clone]</code>, the ubiquitous trait that defines\\n50: //!   [`clone`][Clone::clone], the method for producing a copy of a value.\\n51: //! * <code>[std::cmp]::{[PartialEq], [PartialOrd], [Eq], [Ord]}</code>, the\\n52: //!   comparison traits, which implement the comparison operators and are often\\n53: //!   seen in trait bounds.\\n54: //! * <code>[std::convert]::{[AsRef], [AsMut], [Into], [From]}</code>, generic\\n55: //!   conversions, used by savvy API authors to create overloaded methods.\\n56: //! * <code>[std::default]::[Default]</code>, types that have default values.\\n57: //! * <code>[std::iter]::{[Iterator], [Extend], [IntoIterator], [DoubleEndedIterator], [ExactSizeIterator]}</code>,\\n58: //!   iterators of various\\n59: //!   kinds.\\n60: //! * <code>[std::option]::[Option]::{[self][Option], [Some], [None]}</code>, a\\n61: //!   type which expresses the presence or absence of a value. This type is so\\n62: //!   commonly used, its variants are also exported.\\n63: //! * <code>[std::result]::[Result]::{[self][Result], [Ok], [Err]}</code>, a type\\n64: //!   for functions that may succeed or fail. Like [`Option`], its variants are\\n65: //!   exported as well.\\n66: //! * <code>[std::string]::{[String], [ToString]}</code>, heap-allocated strings.\\n67: //! * <code>[std::vec]::[Vec]</code>, a growable, heap-allocated vector.\\n68: //!\\n69: //! The prelude used in Rust 2021, [`std::prelude::rust_2021`], includes all of the above,\\n70: //! and in addition re-exports:\\n71: //!\\n72: //! * <code>[std::convert]::{[TryFrom], [TryInto]}</code>.\\n73: //! * <code>[std::iter]::[FromIterator]</code>.\\n74: //!\\n75: //! The prelude used in Rust 2024, [`std::prelude::rust_2024`], includes all of the above,\\n76: //! and in addition re-exports:\\n77: //!\\n78: //! * <code>[std::future]::{[Future], [IntoFuture]}</code>.\\n79: //!\\n80: //! //! # The Rust Prelude\n2: //!\n3: //! Rust comes with a variety of things in its standard library. However, if\n4: //! you had to manually import every single thing that you used, it would be\n5: //! very verbose. But importing a lot of things that a program never uses isn't\n6: //! good either. A balance needs to be struck.\n7: //!\n8: //! The *prelude* is the list of things that Rust automatically imports into\n9: //! every Rust program. It's kept as small as possible, and is focused on\n10: //! things, particularly traits, which are used in almost every single Rust\n11: //! program.\n12: //!\n13: //! # Other preludes\n14: //!\n15: //! Preludes can be seen as a pattern to make using multiple types more\n16: //! convenient. As such, you'll find other preludes in the standard library,\n17: //! such as [`std::io::prelude`]. Various libraries in the Rust ecosystem may\n18: //! also define their own preludes.\n19: //!\n20: //! [`std::io::prelude`]: crate::io::prelude\n21: //!\n22: //! The difference between 'the prelude' and these other preludes is that they\n23: //! are not automatically `use`'d, and must be imported manually. This is still\n24: //! easier than importing all of their constituent components.\n25: //!\n26: //! # Prelude contents\n27: //!\n28: //! The items included in the prelude depend on the edition of the crate.\n29: //! The first version of the prelude is used in Rust 2015 and Rust 2018,\n30: //! and lives in [`std::prelude::v1`].\n31: //! [`std::prelude::rust_2015`] and [`std::prelude::rust_2018`] re-export this prelude.\n32: //! It re-exports the following:\n33: //!\n34: //! * <code>[std::marker]::{[Copy], [Send], [Sized], [Sync], [Unpin]}</code>,\n35: //!   marker traits that indicate fundamental properties of types.\n36: //! * <code>[std::ops]::{[Fn], [FnMut], [FnOnce]}</code>, and their analogous\n37: //!   async traits, <code>[std::ops]::{[AsyncFn], [AsyncFnMut], [AsyncFnOnce]}</code>.\n38: //! * <code>[std::ops]::[Drop]</code>, for implementing destructors.\n39: //! * <code>[std::mem]::[drop]</code>, a convenience function for explicitly\n40: //!   dropping a value.\n41: //! * <code>[std::mem]::{[size_of], [size_of_val]}</code>, to get the size of\n42: //!   a type or value.\n43: //! * <code>[std::mem]::{[align_of], [align_of_val]}</code>, to get the\n44: //!   alignment of a type or value.\n45: //! * <code>[std::boxed]::[Box]</code>, a way to allocate values on the heap.\n46: //! * <code>[std::borrow]::[ToOwned]</code>, the conversion trait that defines\n47: //!   [`to_owned`], the generic method for creating an owned type from a\n48: //!   borrowed type.\n49: //! * <code>[std::clone]::[Clone]</code>, the ubiquitous trait that defines\n50: //!   [`clone`][Clone::clone], the method for producing a copy of a value.\n51: //! * <code>[std::cmp]::{[PartialEq], [PartialOrd], [Eq], [Ord]}</code>, the\n52: //!   comparison traits, which implement the comparison operators and are often\n53: //!   seen in trait bounds.\n54: //! * <code>[std::convert]::{[AsRef], [AsMut], [Into], [From]}</code>, generic\n55: //!   conversions, used by savvy API authors to create overloaded methods.\n56: //! * <code>[std::default]::[Default]</code>, types that have default values.\n57: //! * <code>[std::iter]::{[Iterator], [Extend], [IntoIterator], [DoubleEndedIterator], [ExactSizeIterator]}</code>,\n58: //!   iterators of various\n59: //!   kinds.\n60: //! * <code>[std::option]::[Option]::{[self][Option], [Some], [None]}</code>, a\n61: //!   type which expresses the presence or absence of a value. This type is so\n62: //!   commonly used, its variants are also exported.\n63: //! * <code>[std::result]::[Result]::{[self][Result], [Ok], [Err]}</code>, a type\n64: //!   for functions that may succeed or fail. Like [`Option`], its variants are\n65: //!   exported as well.\n66: //! * <code>[std::string]::{[String], [ToString]}</code>, heap-allocated strings.\n67: //! * <code>[std::vec]::[Vec]</code>, a growable, heap-allocated vector.\n68: //!\n69: //! The prelude used in Rust 2021, [`std::prelude::rust_2021`], includes all of the above,\n70: //! and in addition re-exports:\n71: //!\n72: //! * <code>[std::convert]::{[TryFrom], [TryInto]}</code>.\n73: //! * <code>[std::iter]::[FromIterator]</code>.\n74: //!\n75: //! The prelude used in Rust 2024, [`std::prelude::rust_2024`], includes all of the above,\n76: //! and in addition re-exports:\n77: //!\n78: //! * <code>[std::future]::{[Future], [IntoFuture]}</code>.\n79: //!\n80: //! [std::borrow]: crate::borrow\n81: //! [std::boxed]: crate::boxed\n82: //! [std::clone]: crate::clone\n83: //! [std::cmp]: crate::cmp\n84: //! [std::convert]: crate::convert\n85: //! [std::default]: crate::default\n86: //! [std::future]: crate::future\n87: //! [std::iter]: crate::iter\n88: //! [std::marker]: crate::marker\n89: //! [std::mem]: crate::mem\n90: //! [std::ops]: crate::ops\n91: //! [std::option]: crate::option\n92: //! [`std::prelude::v1`]: v1\n93: //! [`std::prelude::rust_2015`]: rust_2015\n94: //! [`std::prelude::rust_2018`]: rust_2018\n95: //! [`std::prelude::rust_2021`]: rust_2021\n96: //! [`std::prelude::rust_2024`]: rust_2024\n97: //! [std::result]: crate::result\n98: //! [std::slice]: crate::slice\n99: //! [std::string]: crate::string\n100: //! [std::vec]: mod@crate::vec\n101: //! [`to_owned`]: crate::borrow::ToOwned::to_owned\n102: //! [book-closures]: ../../book/ch13-01-closures.html\n103: //! Names in modules (both normal `mod`ules and blocks), loop through hygienic parents\n93:         //    (open, not controlled).\n94:         // 3. Standard library prelude (de-facto closed, controlled).\n95:         // (Macro NS)\n96:         // 1-3. Derive helpers (open, not controlled). All ambiguities with other names\n97:         //    are currently reported as errors. They should be higher in priority than preludes\n98:         //    and probably even names in modules according to the \"general principles\" above. They\n99:         //    also should be subject to restricted shadowing because are effectively produced by\n100:         //    derives (you need to resolve the derive first to add helpers into scope), but they\n101:         //    should be available before the derive is expanded for compatibility.\n102:         //    It's mess in general, so we are being conservative for now.\n103:         // 1-3. `macro_rules` (open, not controlled), loop through `macro_rules` scopes. Have higher\n104:         //    priority than prelude macros, but create ambiguities with macros in modules.\n105:         // 1-3. Names in modules (both normal `mod`ules and blocks), loop through hygienic parents\n106:         //    (open, not controlled). Have higher priority than prelude macros, but create\n107:         //    ambiguities with `macro_rules`.\n108:         // 4. `macro_use` prelude (open, the open part is from macro expansions, not controlled).\n109:         // 4a. This is an optimization\n147:                     // used to avoid long scope chains, see the comments on `MacroRulesScopeRef`.\n148:                     // As another consequence of this optimization visitors never observe invocation\n149:                     // scopes for macros that were already expanded.\n150:                     while let MacroRulesScope::Invocation(invoc_id) = macro_rules_scope.get() {\n151:                         if let Some(next_scope) = self.output_macro_rules_scopes.get(&invoc_id) {\n152:                             macro_rules_scope.set(next_scope.get());\n153:                         } else {\n154:                             break;\n155:                         }\n156:                     }\n157:                     true\n158:                 }\n159:                 Scope::Module(..) => true,\n160:                 Scope::MacroUsePrelude => use_prelude || rust_2015,\n161:                 Scope::BuiltinAttrs => true,\n162:                 Scope::ExternPreludeItems | Scope::ExternPreludeFlags => {\n163:                     use_prelude || module_and_extern_prelude || extern_prelude\n164:                 }\n165:                 Scope::ToolPrelude => use_prelude,\n166:                 Scope::StdLibPrelude => use_prelude || ns == MacroNS,\n167:                 Scope::BuiltinTypes => true,\n168:             };\n169: \n170:             if visit {\n171:                 let use_prelude = if use_prelude { UsePrelude::Yes } else { UsePrelude::No };\n172:                 if let
